.navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Thermodynamics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Thermodynamics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Thermodynamics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks"><tbody><tr><th class="sidebar-title" style="background: lavender; font-size: 110%"><a class="mw-selflink selflink">Entropy articles</a></th></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content hlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Introduction_to_entropy" title="Introduction to entropy">Introduction</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_entropy" title="History of entropy">History</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Entropy_(classical_thermodynamics)" title="Entropy (classical thermodynamics)">Classical entropy</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Entropy_(statistical_thermodynamics)" title="Entropy (statistical thermodynamics)">Statistical entropy</a></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1063604349"/><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:EntropySegments" title="Template:EntropySegments"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:EntropySegments" title="Template talk:EntropySegments"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:EntropySegments&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Entropy</b> is a scientific concept, as well as a measurable physical property, that is most commonly associated with a state of disorder, randomness, or uncertainty. The term and the concept are used in diverse fields, from <a href="/wiki/Classical_thermodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical thermodynamics">classical thermodynamics</a>, where it was first recognized, to the microscopic description of nature in <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" title="Statistical physics">statistical physics</a>, and to the principles of <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>. It has found far-ranging applications in <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, in biological systems and their relation to life, in <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_science" title="Atmospheric science">weather science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Information_system" title="Information system">information systems</a> including the transmission of information in <a href="/wiki/Telecommunication" class="mw-redirect" title="Telecommunication">telecommunication</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The thermodynamic concept was referred to by Scottish scientist and engineer <a href="/wiki/William_Rankine" title="William Rankine">William Rankine</a> in 1850 with the names <i>thermodynamic function</i> and <i>heat-potential</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> In 1865, German physicist <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a>, one of the leading founders of the field of thermodynamics, defined it as the quotient of an infinitesimal amount of heat to the instantaneous <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperature</a>. He initially described it as <i>transformation-content</i>, in German <i>Verwandlungsinhalt</i>, and later coined the term <i>entropy</i> from a Greek word for <i>transformation</i>. Referring to microscopic constitution and structure, in 1862, Clausius interpreted the concept as meaning <a href="/wiki/Disgregation" title="Disgregation">disgregation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-brush1976_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brush1976-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A consequence of entropy is that certain processes are irreversible or impossible, aside from the requirement of not violating the <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation of energy</a>, the latter being expressed in the <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">first law of thermodynamics</a>. Entropy is central to the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, which states that the entropy of isolated systems left to spontaneous evolution cannot decrease with time, as they always arrive at a state of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>, where the entropy is highest.
</p><p>Austrian physicist <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a> explained entropy as the measure of the number of possible microscopic arrangements or states of individual atoms and molecules of a system that comply with the macroscopic condition of the system. He thereby introduced the concept of statistical disorder and <a href="/wiki/Probability_distribution" title="Probability distribution">probability distributions</a> into a new field of thermodynamics, called <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a>, and found the link between the microscopic interactions, which fluctuate about an average configuration, to the macroscopically observable behavior, in form of a simple <a href="/wiki/Logarithm" title="Logarithm">logarithmic</a> law, with a <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportionality constant</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>, that has become one of the defining universal constants for the modern <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI).
</p><p>In 1948, <a href="/wiki/Bell_Labs" title="Bell Labs">Bell Labs</a> scientist <a href="/wiki/Claude_Shannon" title="Claude Shannon">Claude Shannon</a> developed similar statistical concepts of measuring microscopic uncertainty and multiplicity to the problem of random losses of information in telecommunication signals. Upon <a href="/wiki/John_von_Neumann" title="John von Neumann">John von Neumann</a>'s suggestion, Shannon named this entity of <i>missing information</i> in analogous manner to its use in statistical mechanics as <i>entropy</i>, and gave birth to the field of <a href="/wiki/Information_theory" title="Information theory">information theory</a>. This description has been identified as a universal definition of the concept of entropy.<sup id="cite_ref-ben-naim_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ben-naim-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clausius.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Clausius.jpg/170px-Clausius.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="202" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/Clausius.jpg/255px-Clausius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/40/Clausius.jpg 2x" data-file-width="275" data-file-height="326" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clausius.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a></span> <span class="nowrap">(1822–1888),</span> originator of the concept of entropy</div></div></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_entropy" title="History of entropy">History of entropy</a></div>
<p>In his 1803 paper, <i>Fundamental Principles of Equilibrium and Movement</i>, the French mathematician <a href="/wiki/Lazare_Carnot" title="Lazare Carnot">Lazare Carnot</a> proposed that in any machine, the accelerations and shocks of the moving parts represent losses of <i>moment of activity</i>; in any natural process there exists an inherent tendency towards the dissipation of useful energy. In 1824, building on that work, Lazare's son, <a href="/wiki/Nicolas_L%C3%A9onard_Sadi_Carnot" title="Nicolas Léonard Sadi Carnot">Sadi Carnot</a>, published <i><a href="/wiki/Reflections_on_the_Motive_Power_of_Fire" title="Reflections on the Motive Power of Fire">Reflections on the Motive Power of Fire</a></i>, which posited that in all heat-engines, whenever "<a href="/wiki/Caloric_theory" title="Caloric theory">caloric</a>" (what is now known as heat) falls through a temperature difference, work or <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">motive power</a> can be produced from the actions of its fall from a hot to cold body. He used an analogy with how water falls in a <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheel</a>. That was an early insight into the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Carnot based his views of heat partially on the early 18th-century "Newtonian hypothesis" that both heat and light were types of indestructible forms of matter, which are attracted and repelled by other matter, and partially on the contemporary views of <a href="/wiki/Count_Rumford" class="mw-redirect" title="Count Rumford">Count Rumford</a>, who showed in 1789 that heat could be created by friction, as when cannon bores are machined.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> Carnot reasoned that if the body of the working substance, such as a body of steam, is returned to its original state at the end of a complete <a href="/wiki/Engine_cycle" class="mw-redirect" title="Engine cycle">engine cycle</a>, "no change occurs in the condition of the working body".
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">first law of thermodynamics</a>, deduced from the heat-friction experiments of <a href="/wiki/James_Joule" class="mw-redirect" title="James Joule">James Joule</a> in 1843, expresses the concept of energy, and its <a href="/wiki/Conservation_of_energy" title="Conservation of energy">conservation</a> in all processes; the first law, however, is unsuitable to separately quantify the effects of <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">friction</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dissipation" title="Dissipation">dissipation</a>.
</p><p>In the 1850s and 1860s, German physicist <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a> objected to the supposition that no change occurs in the working body, and gave that change a mathematical interpretation, by questioning the nature of the inherent loss of usable heat when work is done, e.g., heat produced by friction.<sup id="cite_ref-Clausius_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clausius-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> He described his observations as a dissipative use of energy, resulting in a <i>transformation-content</i> (<i>Verwandlungsinhalt</i> in German), of a <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a> or <a href="/wiki/Working_body" class="mw-redirect" title="Working body">working body</a> of <a href="/wiki/Chemical_species" title="Chemical species">chemical species</a> during a change of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_state" title="Thermodynamic state">state</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clausius_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clausius-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> That was in contrast to earlier views, based on the theories of <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, that heat was an indestructible particle that had mass. Clausius discovered that the non-usable energy increases as steam proceeds from inlet to exhaust in a steam engine. From the prefix <i>en-</i>, as in 'energy', and from the Greek word <i>τροπή</i> [tropē], which is translated in an established lexicon as <i>turning</i> or <i>change</i><sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> and that he rendered in German as <i>Verwandlung</i>, a word often translated into English as <i>transformation</i>, in 1865 Clausius coined the name of that property as <i>entropy</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clausius_German_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clausius_German-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The word was adopted into the English language in 1868.
</p><p>Later, scientists such as <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a>, <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/James_Clerk_Maxwell" title="James Clerk Maxwell">James Clerk Maxwell</a> gave entropy a statistical basis. In 1877, Boltzmann visualized a probabilistic way to measure the entropy of an ensemble of <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas" title="Ideal gas">ideal gas</a> particles, in which he defined entropy as proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Natural_logarithm" title="Natural logarithm">natural logarithm</a> of the number of microstates such a gas could occupy. The <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">proportionality constant</a> in this definition, called the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>, has become one of the defining universal constants for the modern <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (SI). Henceforth, the essential problem in <a href="/wiki/Statistical_thermodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Statistical thermodynamics">statistical thermodynamics</a> has been to determine the distribution of a given amount of energy <i>E</i> over <i>N</i> identical systems. <a href="/wiki/Constantin_Carath%C3%A9odory" title="Constantin Carathéodory">Constantin Carathéodory</a>, a Greek mathematician, linked entropy with a mathematical definition of irreversibility, in terms of trajectories and integrability.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1865, Clausius named the concept of "the differential of a quantity which depends on the configuration of the system," <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/entropy" class="extiw" title="wikt:entropy">entropy</a></i> (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Entropie</i></span>) after the Greek word for 'transformation'.<sup id="cite_ref-Gil399_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gil399-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> He gave "transformational content" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Verwandlungsinhalt</i></span>) as a synonym, paralleling his "thermal and ergonal content" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Wärme- und Werkinhalt</i></span>) as the name of <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy"><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle U}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>U</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle U}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/458a728f53b9a0274f059cd695e067c430956025" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.783ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="U"/></span></a>, but preferring the term <i>entropy</i> as a close parallel of the word <i>energy</i>, as he found the concepts nearly "analogous in their physical significance."<sup id="cite_ref-Gil399_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gil399-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> This term was formed by replacing the root of <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἔργον</span></span> ('ergon', 'work') by that of <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%84%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%80%CE%AE" class="extiw" title="wikt:τροπή">τροπή</a></span></span> ('tropy', 'transformation').<sup id="cite_ref-Clausius_German_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clausius_German-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In more detail, Clausius explained his choice of "entropy" as a name as follows:<sup id="cite_ref-Cooper_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>I prefer going to the ancient languages for the names of important scientific quantities, so that they may mean the same thing in all living tongues. I propose, therefore, to call <i>S</i> the <i>entropy</i> of a body, after the Greek word "transformation". I have designedly coined the word <i>entropy</i> to be similar to energy, for these two quantities are so analogous in their physical significance, that an analogy of denominations seems to me helpful.</p></blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Leon_Cooper" title="Leon Cooper">Leon Cooper</a> added that in this way "he succeeded in coining a word that meant the same thing to everybody: nothing."<sup id="cite_ref-Cooper_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cooper-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_and_descriptions">Definitions and descriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Definitions and descriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1062260506">.mw-parser-output .quotebox{background-color:#F9F9F9;border:1px solid #aaa;box-sizing:border-box;padding:10px;font-size:88%;max-width:100%}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft{margin:.5em 1.4em .8em 0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright{margin:.5em 0 .8em 1.4em}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.centered{overflow:hidden;position:relative;margin:.5em auto .8em auto}.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatleft span,.mw-parser-output .quotebox.floatright span{font-style:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox>blockquote{margin:0;padding:0;border-left:0;font-family:inherit;font-size:inherit}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-title{background-color:#F9F9F9;text-align:center;font-size:110%;font-weight:bold}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote>:first-child{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote:last-child>:last-child{margin-bottom:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:before{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" “ ";vertical-align:-45%;line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox-quote.quoted:after{font-family:"Times New Roman",serif;font-weight:bold;font-size:large;color:gray;content:" ” ";line-height:0}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .left-aligned{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .right-aligned{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .center-aligned{text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quote-title,.mw-parser-output .quotebox .quotebox-quote{display:block}.mw-parser-output .quotebox cite{display:block;font-style:normal}@media screen and (max-width:640px){.mw-parser-output .quotebox{width:100%!important;margin:0 0 .8em!important;float:none!important}}</style><div class="quotebox pullquote floatright" style="width:30em; ;">
<blockquote class="quotebox-quote left-aligned" style="">
<p>Any method involving the notion of entropy, the very existence of which depends on the second law of thermodynamics, will doubtless seem to many far-fetched, and may repel beginners as obscure and difficult of comprehension.
</p>
</blockquote>
<p><cite class="left-aligned" style=""><a href="/wiki/Willard_Gibbs" class="mw-redirect" title="Willard Gibbs">Willard Gibbs</a>, <i>Graphical Methods in the Thermodynamics of Fluids</i><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup></cite>
</p>
</div>
<p>The concept of entropy is described by two principal approaches, the macroscopic perspective of <a href="/wiki/Classical_thermodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical thermodynamics">classical thermodynamics</a>, and the microscopic description central to <a href="/wiki/Statistical_mechanics" title="Statistical mechanics">statistical mechanics</a>. The classical approach defines entropy in terms of macroscopically measurable physical properties, such as bulk mass, volume, pressure, and temperature. The statistical definition of entropy defines it in terms of the statistics of the motions of the microscopic constituents of a system – modeled at first classically, e.g. Newtonian particles constituting a gas, and later quantum-mechanically (photons, <a href="/wiki/Phonons" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonons">phonons</a>, spins, etc.). The two approaches form a consistent, unified view of the same phenomenon as expressed in the second law of thermodynamics, which has found universal applicability to physical processes.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_variables_and_functions_of_state">State variables and functions of state</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: State variables and functions of state">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Many <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_properties" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermodynamic properties">thermodynamic properties</a> are defined by physical variables that define a state of <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>; these are <i>state variables</i>. State variables depend only on the equilibrium condition, not on the path evolution to that state. State variables can be <a href="/wiki/Functions_of_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Functions of state">functions of state</a>, also called <i>state functions</i>, in a sense that one state variable is a <a href="/wiki/Function_(mathematics)" title="Function (mathematics)">mathematical function</a> of other state variables. Often, if some properties of a system are determined, they are sufficient to determine the state of the system and thus other properties' values. For example, temperature and pressure of a given quantity of gas determine its state, and thus also its volume via the <a href="/wiki/Ideal_gas_law" title="Ideal gas law">ideal gas law</a>. A system composed of a pure substance of a single <a href="/wiki/Phase_(matter)" title="Phase (matter)">phase</a> at a particular uniform temperature and pressure is determined, and is thus a particular state, and has not only a particular volume but also a specific entropy.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The fact that entropy is a function of state makes it useful. In the Carnot cycle, the working fluid returns to the same state that it had at the start of the cycle, hence the change or <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">line integral</a> of any state function, such as entropy, over this reversible cycle is zero.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reversible_process">Reversible process</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Reversible process">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Total entropy may be conserved during a <a href="/wiki/Reversible_process_(thermodynamics)" title="Reversible process (thermodynamics)">reversible process</a>. The entropy change <i><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle dS}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>S</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle dS}</annotation>
  </semantics>

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d97924da358fdb47b61a8e962868779ddcbfe121" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:10.676ex; height:4.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle dS={\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}"/></span>. A reversible process is a <a href="/wiki/Quasistatic_process" title="Quasistatic process">quasistatic</a> one that deviates only infinitesimally from thermodynamic equilibrium and avoids friction or other dissipation. Any process that happens quickly enough to deviate from thermal equilibrium cannot be reversible, total entropy increases, and the potential for maximum work to be done in the process is also lost.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> For example, in the <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a>, while the heat flow from the hot reservoir to the cold reservoir represents an increase in entropy, the work output, if reversibly and perfectly stored in some energy storage mechanism, represents a decrease in entropy that could be used to operate the heat engine in <b>reverse</b> and return to the previous state; thus the <i>total</i> entropy change may still be zero at all times if the entire process is reversible. An irreversible process increases the total entropy of system and surroundings.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carnot_cycle">Carnot cycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Carnot cycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The concept of entropy arose from <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a>'s study of the <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a> that is a <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_cycle" title="Thermodynamic cycle">thermodynamic cycle</a> performed by a Carnot heat engine as a reversible heat engine.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> In a Carnot cycle, heat <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> is absorbed isothermally at temperature <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>H</sub></span> from a 'hot' reservoir (in the isothermal expansion stage) and given up isothermally as heat <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>C</sub></span> to a 'cold' reservoir at <span class="texhtml"><i>T</i><sub>C</sub></span> (in the isothermal compression stage). According to <a href="/wiki/Carnot%27s_theorem_(thermodynamics)" title="Carnot&#39;s theorem (thermodynamics)">Carnot's principle or theorem</a>, <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">work</a> from a heat engine with two thermal reservoirs can be produced only when there is a temperature difference between these reservoirs, and for reversible engines which are mostly and equally efficient among all heat engines for a given thermal reservoir pair, the work is a function of the reservoir temperatures and the heat absorbed to the engine <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> (heat engine work output = <a href="/wiki/Heat_engine#Efficiency" title="Heat engine">heat engine efficiency</a> × heat to the engine, where the efficiency is a function of the reservoir temperatures for reversible heat engines). Carnot did not distinguish between <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>C</sub></span>, since he was using the incorrect hypothesis that <a href="/wiki/Caloric_theory" title="Caloric theory">caloric theory</a> was valid, and hence heat was conserved (the incorrect assumption that <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> and <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>C</sub></span> were equal in magnitude) when, in fact, <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> is greater than the magnitude of <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>C</sub></span> in magnitude.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Through the efforts of Clausius and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Kelvin" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Kelvin">Kelvin</a>, it is now known that the work done by a reversible heat engine is the product of the Carnot efficiency (it is the efficiency of all reversible heat engines with the same thermal reservoir pairs according to the <a href="/wiki/Carnot%27s_theorem_(thermodynamics)" title="Carnot&#39;s theorem (thermodynamics)">Carnot's theorem</a>) and the heat absorbed from the hot reservoir:
</p>

<p style="margin:0; font-size:1pt;">&#160;</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
</div>
</td>
<td style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;" class="nowrap"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(<span id="math_1" class="reference nourlexpansion" style="font-weight:bold;">1</span>)</b></p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Here <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle W}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>W</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/54a9c4c547f4d6111f81946cad242b18298d70b7" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:2.435ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="W"/></span> is work done by the Carnot heat engine, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle Q_{\text{H}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">

          <mi>Q</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mtext>H</mtext>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle Q_{\text{H}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/215f330aa0c9ca32a10c4a8410224ecfba72b713" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:3.303ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="Q_{\text{H}}"/></span> is heat to the engine from the hot reservoir, and  <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle -{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}Q_{\text{H}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <msub>
              <mi>T</mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>C</mtext>
              </mrow>
            </msub>
            <msub>
              <mi>T</mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mtext>H</mtext>
              </mrow>
            </msub>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <msub>
          <mi>Q</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mtext>H</mtext>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle -{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}Q_{\text{H}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b10b5833a2ca1bb7c3e50ae1d4175d4690ce6d30" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:8.77ex; height:5.676ex;" alt="{\displaystyle -{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}Q_{\text{H}}}"/></span> is heat to the cold reservoir from the engine. To derive the <i>Carnot efficiency</i>, which is <span class="texhtml">1 − <i>T</i><sub>C</sub>/<i>T</i><sub>H</sub></span> (a number less than one), Kelvin had to evaluate the ratio of the work output to the heat absorbed during the isothermal expansion with the help of the Carnot–Clapeyron equation, which contained an unknown function called the Carnot function. The possibility that the Carnot function could be the temperature as measured from a zero point of temperature was suggested by <a href="/wiki/James_Prescott_Joule" title="James Prescott Joule">Joule</a> in a letter to Kelvin. This allowed Kelvin to establish his absolute temperature scale.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> It is also known that the net work <span class="texhtml"><i>W</i></span> produced by the system in one cycle is the net heat absorbed, which is the sum (or difference of the magnitudes) of the <a href="/wiki/Heat" title="Heat">heat</a> <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span> &gt; 0 absorbed from the hot reservoir and the waste heat <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>C</sub></span> &lt; 0 given off to the cold reservoir:<sup id="cite_ref-PlanckBook_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlanckBook-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table style="border-collapse:collapse; background:none; margin:0; border:none;">
<tbody><tr>

<td style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;" class="nowrap"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(<span id="math_2" class="reference nourlexpansion" style="font-weight:bold;">2</span>)</b></p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Since the latter is valid over the entire cycle, this gave Clausius the hint that at each stage of the cycle, work and heat would not be equal, but rather their difference would be the change of a state function that would vanish upon completion of the cycle. The state function was called the <a href="/wiki/Internal_energy" title="Internal energy">internal energy</a>, that is central to the <a href="/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics" title="First law of thermodynamics">first law of thermodynamics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Clausius1867_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Clausius1867-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Now equating (<b><a href="#math_1">1</a></b>) and (<b><a href="#math_2">2</a></b>) gives, for the engine per Carnot cycle,<sup id="cite_ref-FermiBook_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FermiBook-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PlanckBook_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PlanckBook-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><br />
</p>

<td style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;" class="nowrap"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(<span id="math_3" class="reference nourlexpansion" style="font-weight:bold;">3</span>)</b></p>
</td></tr></tbody></table><p>
This implies that there is a function of state whose change is <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i>/<i>T</i></span> and this state function is conserved over a complete Carnot cycle, like other state function such as the internal energy. Clausius called this state function <i>entropy</i>. One can see that entropy was discovered through mathematics rather than through laboratory experimental results.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> It is a mathematical construct and has no easy physical analogy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> This makes the concept somewhat obscure or abstract, akin to how the concept of energy arose.<style Please do not remove this message until <a href="/wiki/Template:POV#When_to_remove" title="Template:POV">conditions to do so are met</a>.</span>  <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">November 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p> This equation shows an entropy change per Carnot cycle is zero. In fact, an entropy change in the both thermal reservoirs per Carnot cycle is also zero since that change is simply expressed by reverting the sign of each term in the equation (<b><a href="#math_3">3</a></b>) according to the fact that, for example, for heat transfer from the hot reservoir to the engine, the engine receives the heat while the hot reservoir loses the same amount of the heat;
</p><p><br />
</p>

<td style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;" class="nowrap"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(<span id="math_4" class="reference nourlexpansion" style="font-weight:bold;">4</span>)</b></p>

<p>where we denote an entropy change for a thermal reservoir by <span class="texhtml">Δ<i>S</i><sub><i>r,i</i></sub> = - <i>Q</i><sub><i>i</i></sub>/<i>T</i><sub><i>i</i></sub></span>, for <i>i</i> as either H (Hot reservoir) or C (Cold reservoir), by considering the abovementioned signal convention of heat for the engine.
</p><p>Clausius then asked what would happen if less work is produced by the system than that predicted by Carnot's principle for the same thermal reservoir pair and the same heat transfer from the hot reservoir to the engine <span class="texhtml"><i>Q</i><sub>H</sub></span>. In this case, the right-hand side of the equation (<b><a href="#math_1">1</a></b>) would be the upper bound of the work output by the system, and the equation would now be converted into an inequality<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle W&lt;\left(1-{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}\right)Q_{\text{H}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        <mo>&lt;</mo>
        <mrow>
          <mo>(</mo>
          <mrow>
            <mn>1</mn>
            <mo>&#x2212;<!-- − --></mo>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
                <msub>
                  <mi>T</mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mtext>C</mtext>
                  </mrow>
                </msub>
                <msub>
                  <mi>T</mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mtext>H</mtext>
                  </mrow>
                </msub>
              </mfrac>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
          <mo>)</mo>
        </mrow>
        <msub>
          <mi>Q</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mtext>H</mtext>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle W&lt;\left(1-{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}\right)Q_{\text{H}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></div><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f9fa0959c4857413a137bb560b9c0e2c88af976b" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:20.306ex; height:6.176ex;" alt="{\displaystyle W&lt;\left(1-{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}\right)Q_{\text{H}}}"/></div>When the equation (<b><a href="#math_2">2</a></b>) is used to express the work as a net or total heat exchanged in a cycle, we get<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle Q_{\text{H}}+Q_{\text{C}}&lt;\left(1-{\frac {T_{\text{C}}}{T_{\text{H}}}}\right)Q_{\text{H}}}">

<td style="vertical-align:middle; border:none; padding:0.08em;" class="nowrap"><p style="margin:0pt;"><b>(<span id="math_5" class="reference nourlexpansion" style="font-weight:bold;">5</span>)</b></p>
</td></tr></tbody></table><p>telling that the magnitude of the entropy earned by the cold reservoir is greater than the entropy lost by the hot reservoir. The net entropy change in the engine per its thermodynamic cycle is zero, so the net entropy change in the engine and both the thermal reservoirs per cycle increases if work produced by the engine is less than the work achieved by a Carnot engine in the equation (<b><a href="#math_1">1</a></b>).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a> and Carnot efficiency as shown in the equation (<b><a href="#math_1">1</a></b>) are useful because they define the upper bound of the possible work output and the efficiency of any classical thermodynamic heat engine. Other cycles, such as the <a href="/wiki/Otto_cycle" title="Otto cycle">Otto cycle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diesel_cycle" title="Diesel cycle">Diesel cycle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brayton_cycle" title="Brayton cycle">Brayton cycle</a>, can be analyzed from the standpoint of the Carnot cycle. Any machine or cyclic process that converts heat to work and is claimed to produce an efficiency greater than the Carnot efficiency is not viable because it violates <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">the second law of thermodynamics</a>.
</p><p>For very small numbers of particles in the system, statistical thermodynamics must be used. The efficiency of devices such as photovoltaic cells requires an analysis from the standpoint of quantum mechanics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_thermodynamics">Classical thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Classical thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(classical_thermodynamics)" title="Entropy (classical thermodynamics)">Entropy (classical thermodynamics)</a></div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks wraplinks" style="width:18em;"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-above" style="background:lavender; font-size:120%;">
<a href="/wiki/Conjugate_variables_(thermodynamics)" title="Conjugate variables (thermodynamics)">Conjugate variables<br />of thermodynamics</a></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content" style="padding:2px;">
<table class="wikitable" style="width:100%;border-collapse:collapse;border-spacing:0px 0px;border:none;margin:1px 0 0 0;"><tbody><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td> <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">Pressure</a></td><td> <a href="/wiki/Volume_(thermodynamics)" title="Volume (thermodynamics)">Volume</a></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td> (<a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">Stress</a>)</td><td> (<a href="/wiki/Deformation_(physics)#Strain" title="Deformation (physics)">Strain</a>)</td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td> <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">Temperature</a></td><td> <a class="mw-selflink selflink">Entropy</a></td></tr><tr style="vertical-align:top"><td> <a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">Chemical potential</a></td><td> <a href="/wiki/Particle_number" title="Particle number">Particle number</a></td></tr></tbody></table></td>
</tr></tbody></table>
<p>The thermodynamic definition of entropy was developed in the early 1850s by <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Clausius" title="Rudolf Clausius">Rudolf Clausius</a> and essentially describes how to measure the entropy of an <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated system</a> in <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a> with its parts. Clausius created the term entropy as an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties" title="Intensive and extensive properties">extensive</a> thermodynamic variable that was shown to be useful in characterizing the <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a>. Heat transfer in the isotherm steps (isothermal expansion and isothermal compression) of the Carnot cycle was found to be proportional to the temperature of a system (known as its <a href="/wiki/Absolute_temperature" class="mw-redirect" title="Absolute temperature">absolute temperature</a>). This relationship was expressed in an increment of entropy that is equal to incremental heat transfer divided by temperature. Entropy was found to vary in the thermodynamic cycle but eventually returned to the same value at the end of every cycle. Thus it was found to be a <a href="/wiki/Function_of_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Function of state">function of state</a>, specifically a thermodynamic state of the system.
</p><p>While Clausius based his definition on a reversible process, there are also irreversible processes that change entropy. Following the <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>, entropy of an isolated <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">system</a> always increases for irreversible processes. The difference between an isolated system and closed system is that energy may <i>not</i> flow to and from an isolated system, but energy flow to and from a closed system is possible. Nevertheless, for both closed and isolated systems, and indeed, also in open systems, irreversible thermodynamics processes may occur.
</p><p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Clausius_theorem" title="Clausius theorem">Clausius equality</a>, for a reversible cyclic process: <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle \oint {\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}=0}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mo>&#x222E;<!-- ∮ --></mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>&#x03B4;<!-- δ --></mi>
              <msub>
                <mi>Q</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mtext>rev</mtext>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
            </mrow>
            <mi>T</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mn>0</mn>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle \oint {\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}=0}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/81163967df75f275ff81d655c5520bda39f247ed" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:10.928ex; height:4.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle \oint {\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}=0}"/></span>. This means the line integral <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle \int _{L}{\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <msub>
          <mo>&#x222B;<!-- ∫ --></mo>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mi>L</mi>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>&#x03B4;<!-- δ --></mi>
              <msub>
                <mi>Q</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mtext>rev</mtext>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
            </mrow>
            <mi>T</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle \int _{L}{\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/310fa14f7bb3042eee3baa1bd1784cc90a46bdbf" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:7.698ex; height:4.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle \int _{L}{\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}"/></span> is <a href="/wiki/State_function" title="State function">path-independent</a>.
</p><p>So we can define a state function <span class="texhtml mvar" style="font-style:italic;">S</span> called entropy, which satisfies <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle dS={\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>d</mi>
        <mi>S</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
          <mfrac>
            <mrow>
              <mi>&#x03B4;<!-- δ --></mi>
              <msub>
                <mi>Q</mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mtext>rev</mtext>
                </mrow>
              </msub>
            </mrow>
            <mi>T</mi>
          </mfrac>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\textstyle dS={\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d97924da358fdb47b61a8e962868779ddcbfe121" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -1.171ex; width:10.676ex; height:4.009ex;" alt="{\textstyle dS={\frac {\delta Q_{\text{rev}}}{T}}}"/></span>.
</p><p>To find the entropy difference between any two states of a system, the integral must be evaluated for some reversible path between the initial and final states.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Since entropy is a state function, the entropy change of the system for an irreversible path is the same as for a reversible path between the same two states.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> However, the heat transferred to or from, and the entropy change of, the surroundings is different.
</p><p>We can only obtain the change of entropy by integrating the above formula. To obtain the absolute value of the entropy, we need the <a href="/wiki/Third_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Third law of thermodynamics">third law of thermodynamics</a>, which states that <i>S</i> = 0 at <a href="/wiki/Absolute_zero" title="Absolute zero">absolute zero</a> for perfect crystals.
</p><p>From a macroscopic perspective, in <a href="/wiki/Classical_thermodynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical thermodynamics">classical thermodynamics</a> the entropy is interpreted as a <a href="/wiki/State_function" title="State function">state function</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a>: that is, a property depending only on the current state of the system, independent of how that state came to be achieved. In any process where the system gives up energy Δ<i>E</i>, and its entropy falls by Δ<i>S</i>, a quantity at least <i>T</i><sub>R</sub> Δ<i>S</i> of that energy must be given up to the system's surroundings as heat (<i>T</i><sub>R</sub> is the temperature of the system's external surroundings). Otherwise the process cannot go forward. In classical thermodynamics, the entropy of a system is defined only if it is in physical <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_equilibrium" title="Thermodynamic equilibrium">thermodynamic equilibrium</a>. (But chemical equilibrium is not required: the entropy of a mixture of two moles of hydrogen and one mole of oxygen at 1 bar pressure and 298 K is well-defined.)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Statistical_mechanics">Statistical mechanics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Statistical mechanics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"/><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(statistical_thermodynamics)" title="Entropy (statistical thermodynamics)">Entropy (statistical thermodynamics)</a></div>
<p>The statistical definition was developed by <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Boltzmann" title="Ludwig Boltzmann">Ludwig Boltzmann</a> in the 1870s by analyzing the statistical behavior of the microscopic components of the system. Boltzmann showed that this definition of entropy was equivalent to the thermodynamic entropy to within a constant factor—known as the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>. In short, the thermodynamic definition of entropy provides the experimental verification of entropy, while the statistical definition of entropy extends the concept, providing an explanation and a deeper understanding of its nature.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(statistical_thermodynamics)" title="Entropy (statistical thermodynamics)">interpretation of entropy in statistical mechanics</a> is the measure of uncertainty, disorder, or <i>mixedupness</i> in the phrase of <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Gibbs</a>, which remains about a system after its observable macroscopic properties, such as temperature, pressure and volume, have been taken into account. For a given set of macroscopic variables, the entropy measures the degree to which the probability of the system is spread out over different possible <a href="/wiki/Microstate_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Microstate (statistical mechanics)">microstates</a>. In contrast to the macrostate, which characterizes plainly observable average quantities, a microstate specifies all molecular details about the system including the position and velocity of every molecule. The more such states are available to the system with appreciable probability, the greater the entropy. In statistical mechanics, entropy is a measure of the number of ways a system can be arranged, often taken to be a measure of "disorder" (the higher the entropy, the higher the disorder).<sup id="cite_ref-McH_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McH-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sethna78_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sethna78-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> This definition describes the entropy as being proportional to the natural logarithm of the number of possible microscopic configurations of the individual atoms and molecules of the system (<a href="/wiki/Microstate_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Microstate (statistical mechanics)">microstates</a>) that could cause the observed macroscopic state (<a href="/wiki/Macrostate" class="mw-redirect" title="Macrostate">macrostate</a>) of the system. The constant of proportionality is the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_constant" title="Boltzmann constant">Boltzmann constant</a>.
</p><p>The Boltzmann constant, and therefore entropy, have <a href="/wiki/Dimension_(physics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Dimension (physics)">dimensions</a> of energy divided by temperature, which has a unit of <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">joules</a> per <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">kelvin</a> (J⋅K<sup>−1</sup>) in the <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">International System of Units</a> (or kg⋅m<sup>2</sup>⋅s<sup>−2</sup>⋅K<sup>−1</sup> in terms of base units). The entropy of a substance is usually given as an <a href="/wiki/Intensive_and_extensive_properties#Intensive_properties" title="Intensive and extensive properties">intensive property</a>&#160;&#8211;&#32;either entropy per unit <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">mass</a> (SI unit: J⋅K<sup>−1</sup>⋅kg<sup>−1</sup>) or entropy per unit <a href="/wiki/Amount_of_substance" title="Amount of substance">amount of substance</a> (SI unit: J⋅K<sup>−1</sup>⋅mol<sup>−1</sup>).
</p><p>Specifically, entropy is a <a href="/wiki/Logarithmic_scale" title="Logarithmic scale">logarithmic</a> measure of the number of system states with significant probability of being occupied:
</p>

    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>log</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \log }</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/79e4debd0ab1c6ce342d0172a7643733305c37bc" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.972ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="\log "/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Matrix_logarithm" class="mw-redirect" title="Matrix logarithm">matrix logarithm</a>. This density matrix formulation is not needed in cases of thermal equilibrium so long as the basis states are chosen to be energy eigenstates. For most practical purposes, this can be taken as the fundamental definition of entropy since all other formulas for <i>S</i> can be mathematically derived from it, but not vice versa.
</p><p>In what has been called <i>the fundamental assumption of statistical thermodynamics</i> or <i><a href="/wiki/Fundamental_postulate_of_statistical_mechanics" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamental postulate of statistical mechanics">the fundamental postulate in statistical mechanics</a></i>, among system microstates of the same energy (<a href="/wiki/Degenerate_energy_levels" title="Degenerate energy levels">degenerate</a> microstates) each microstate is assumed to be populated with equal probability; this assumption is usually justified for an isolated system in equilibrium.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Then for an isolated system <i>p</i><sub><i>i</i></sub> = 1/Ω, where Ω is the number of microstates whose energy equals the system's energy, and the previous equation reduces to
</p>
<dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"  alttext="{\displaystyle S=k_{\mathrm {B} }\log \Omega .}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>S</mi>
        <mo>=</mo>
        <msub>
          <mi>k</mi>
          <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="normal">B</mi>
            </mrow>
          </mrow>
        </msub>
        <mi>log</mi>
        <mo>&#x2061;<!-- ⁡ --></mo>
        <mi mathvariant="normal">&#x03A9;<!-- Ω --></mi>
        <mo>.</mo>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S=k_{\mathrm {B} }\log \Omega .}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4e64b4217e9802e114528612164a9858688193da" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:13.276ex; height:2.509ex;" alt="{\displaystyle S=k_{\mathrm {B} }\log \Omega .}"/></span></dd></dl>
<p>In thermodynamics, such a system is one in which the volume, number of molecules, and internal energy are fixed (the <a href="/wiki/Microcanonical_ensemble" title="Microcanonical ensemble">microcanonical ensemble</a>).
</p><p>For a given thermodynamic system, the <i>excess entropy</i> is defined as the entropy minus that of an ideal gas at the same density and temperature, a quantity that is always negative because an ideal gas is maximally disordered.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> This concept plays an important role in liquid-state theory. For instance, Rosenfeld's excess-entropy scaling principle<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> states that reduced transport coefficients throughout the two-dimensional phase diagram are functions uniquely determined by the excess entropy.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The most general interpretation of entropy is as a measure of the extent of uncertainty about a system. The <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Equilibrium state">equilibrium state</a> of a system maximizes the entropy because it does not reflect all information about the initial conditions, except for the conserved variables. This uncertainty is not of the everyday subjective kind, but rather the uncertainty inherent to the experimental method and interpretative model.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The interpretative model has a central role in determining entropy. The qualifier "for a given set of macroscopic variables" above has deep implications: if two observers use different sets of macroscopic variables, they see different entropies. For example, if observer A uses the variables <i>U</i>, <i>V</i> and <i>W</i>, and observer B uses <i>U</i>, <i>V</i>, <i>W</i>, <i>X</i>, then, by changing <i>X</i>, observer B can cause an effect that looks like a violation of the second law of thermodynamics to observer A. In other words: the set of macroscopic variables one chooses must include everything that may change in the experiment, otherwise one might see decreasing entropy.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Entropy can be defined for any <a href="/wiki/Markov_process" class="mw-redirect" title="Markov process">Markov processes</a> with <a href="/wiki/Reversible_dynamics" class="mw-redirect" title="Reversible dynamics">reversible dynamics</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Detailed_balance" title="Detailed balance">detailed balance</a> property.
</p><p>In Boltzmann's 1896 <i>Lectures on Gas Theory</i>, he showed that this expression gives a measure of entropy for systems of atoms and molecules in the gas phase, thus providing a measure for the entropy of classical thermodynamics.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entropy_of_a_system">Entropy of a system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Entropy of a system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:System_boundary.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/System_boundary.svg/220px-System_boundary.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/System_boundary.svg/330px-System_boundary.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/System_boundary.svg/440px-System_boundary.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="931" data-file-height="818" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:System_boundary.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam,_imperial_units.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg/220px-Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg/330px-Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg/440px-Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam%2C_imperial_units.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1302" data-file-height="1325" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Temperature-entropy_chart_for_steam,_imperial_units.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Temperature%E2%80%93entropy_diagram" title="Temperature–entropy diagram">temperature–entropy diagram</a> for steam. The vertical axis represents uniform temperature, and the horizontal axis represents specific entropy. Each dark line on the graph represents constant pressure, and these form a mesh with light gray lines of constant volume. (Dark-blue is liquid water, light-blue is liquid-steam mixture, and faint-blue is steam. Grey-blue represents supercritical liquid water.)</div></div></div>
<p>Entropy arises directly from the <a href="/wiki/Carnot_cycle" title="Carnot cycle">Carnot cycle</a>. It can also be described as the reversible heat divided by temperature. Entropy is a fundamental function of state.
</p><p>In a <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a>, pressure and temperature tend to become uniform over time because the <a href="/wiki/Equilibrium_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Equilibrium state">equilibrium state</a> has higher <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a> (more possible <a href="/wiki/Combination" title="Combination">combinations</a> of <a href="/wiki/Microstate_(statistical_mechanics)" title="Microstate (statistical mechanics)">microstates</a>) than any other state.
</p><p>As an example, for a glass of ice water in air at <a href="/wiki/Room_temperature" title="Room temperature">room temperature</a>, the difference in temperature between the warm room (the surroundings) and the cold glass of ice and water (the system and not part of the room) decreases as portions of the <a href="/wiki/Thermal_energy" title="Thermal energy">thermal energy</a> from the warm surroundings spread to the cooler system of ice and water. Over time the temperature of the glass and its contents and the temperature of the room become equal. In other words, the entropy of the room has decreased as some of its energy has been dispersed to the ice and water, of which the entropy has increased.
</p><p>However, as calculated in the example, the entropy of the system of ice and water has increased more than the entropy of the surrounding room has decreased. In an <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated system</a> such as the room and ice water taken together, the dispersal of energy from warmer to cooler always results in a net increase in entropy. Thus, when the "universe" of the room and ice water system has reached a temperature equilibrium, the entropy change from the initial state is at a maximum. The entropy of the <a href="/wiki/Thermodynamic_system" title="Thermodynamic system">thermodynamic system</a> is a measure of how far the equalization has progressed.
</p><p>Thermodynamic entropy is a non-conserved <a href="/wiki/State_function" title="State function">state function</a> that is of great importance in the sciences of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemistry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McH_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McH-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Wiley91_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiley91-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Historically, the concept of entropy evolved to explain why some processes (permitted by conservation laws) occur spontaneously while their <a href="/wiki/T-symmetry" title="T-symmetry">time reversals</a> (also permitted by conservation laws) do not; systems tend to progress in the direction of increasing entropy.<sup id="cite_ref-McQuarrie817_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McQuarrie817-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> For <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated systems</a>, entropy never decreases.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiley91_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiley91-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> This fact has several important consequences in science: first, it prohibits "<a href="/wiki/Perpetual_motion" title="Perpetual motion">perpetual motion</a>" machines; and second, it implies the <a href="/wiki/Entropy_(arrow_of_time)" class="mw-redirect" title="Entropy (arrow of time)">arrow of entropy</a> has the same direction as the <a href="/wiki/Arrow_of_time" title="Arrow of time">arrow of time</a>. Increases in the total entropy of system and surroundings correspond to irreversible changes, because some energy is expended as waste heat, limiting the amount of work a system can do.<sup id="cite_ref-McH_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McH-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sethna78_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sethna78-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-OxSci_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OxSci-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Unlike many other functions of state, entropy cannot be directly observed but must be calculated. Absolute <a href="/wiki/Standard_molar_entropy" title="Standard molar entropy">standard molar entropy</a> of a substance can be calculated from the measured temperature dependence of its <a href="/wiki/Heat_capacity" title="Heat capacity">heat capacity</a>. The molar entropy of ions is obtained as a difference in entropy from a reference state defined as zero entropy. The <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> states that the entropy of an <a href="/wiki/Isolated_system" title="Isolated system">isolated system</a> must increase or remain constant. Therefore, entropy is not a conserved quantity: for example, in an isolated system with non-uniform temperature, heat might irreversibly flow and the temperature become more uniform such that entropy increases.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> Chemical reactions cause changes in entropy and system entropy, in conjunction with <a href="/wiki/Enthalpy" title="Enthalpy">enthalpy</a>, plays an important role in determining in which direction a chemical reaction spontaneously proceeds.
</p><p>One dictionary definition of entropy is that it is "a measure of thermal energy per unit temperature that is not available for useful work" in a cyclic process. For instance, a substance at uniform temperature is at maximum entropy and cannot drive a heat engine. A substance at non-uniform temperature is at a lower entropy (than if the heat distribution is allowed to even out) and some of the thermal energy can drive a heat engine.
</p><p>A special case of entropy increase, the <a href="/wiki/Entropy_of_mixing" title="Entropy of mixing">entropy of mixing</a>, occurs when two or more different substances are mixed. If the substances are at the same temperature and pressure, there is no net exchange of heat or work – the entropy change is entirely due to the mixing of the different substances. At a statistical mechanical level, this results due to the change in available volume per particle with mixing.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Equivalence_of_definitions">Equivalence of definitions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Equivalence of definitions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

However, the equivalence between the Gibbs entropy formula and the thermodynamic definition of entropy is not a fundamental thermodynamic relation but rather a consequence of the form of the <a href="/wiki/Boltzmann_distribution#Generalized_Boltzmann_distribution" title="Boltzmann distribution">generalized Boltzmann distribution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, it has been shown that the definitions of entropy in statistical mechanics is the only entropy that is equivalent to the classical thermodynamics entropy under the following postulates:<sup id="cite_ref-Gao2022_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gao2022-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div><ol><li>The probability density function is proportional to some function of the ensemble parameters and random variables.</li><li>Thermodynamic state functions are described by ensemble averages of random variables.</li><li>At infinite temperature, all the microstates have the same probability.</li></ol></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_law_of_thermodynamics">Second law of thermodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Entropy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Second law of thermodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a> requires that, in general, the total entropy of any system does not decrease other than by increasing the entropy of some other system. Hence, in a system isolated from its environment, the entropy of that system tends not to decrease. It follows that heat cannot flow from a colder body to a hotter body without the application of work to the colder body. Secondly, it is impossible for any device operating on a cycle to produce net work from a single temperature reservoir; the production of net work requires flow of heat from a hotter reservoir to a colder reservoir, or a single expanding reservoir undergoing <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_cooling" class="mw-redirect" title="Adiabatic cooling">adiabatic cooling</a>, which performs <a href="/wiki/Adiabatic_process" title="Adiabatic process">adiabatic work</a>. As a result, there is no possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_motion" title="Perpetual motion">perpetual motion</a> machine. It follows that a reduction in the increase of entropy in a specified process, such as a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">chemical reaction</a>, means that it is energetically more efficient.
</p><p>It follows from the second law of thermodynamics that the entropy of a system that is not isolated may decrease. An <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioner" class="mw-redirect" title="Air conditioner">air conditioner</a>, for example, may cool the air in a room, thus reducing the entropy of the air of that system. The heat expelled from the room (the system), which the air conditioner transports and discharges to the outside air, always makes a bigger contribution to the entropy of the environment than the decrease of the entropy of the air of that system. Thus, the total of entropy of the room plus the entropy of the environment increases, in agreement with the second law of thermodynamics.
</p><p>In mechanics, the second law in conjunction with the <a href="/wiki/Fundamental_thermodynamic_relation" title="Fundamental thermodynamic relation">fundamental thermodynamic relation</a> places limits on a system's ability to do <a href="/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)" title="Work (thermodynamics)">useful work</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Daintith_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daintith-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The entropy change of a system at temperature <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle T}">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
      <mstyle displaystyle="false" scriptlevel="0">
        <mi>T</mi>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>

More explicitly, an energy <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" alttext="{\textstyle T_{R}S}">
